<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">United States Environmental Protection Agency</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"EPA" and "Environmental Protection Agency" redirect here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/EPA_(disambiguation)" title="EPA (disambiguation)">EPA (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

<span class="url"><a class="external text" href="http://www.epa.gov" rel="nofollow">www<wbr/>.epa<wbr/>.gov</a></span></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>Environmental Protection Agency</b> is an <a href="/wiki/Independent_agencies_of_the_United_States_government" title="Independent agencies of the United States government">independent agency</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_federal_government" title="United States federal government">United States federal government</a> for <a href="/wiki/Environmental_protection" title="Environmental protection">environmental protection</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> President <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a> proposed the establishment of EPA on July 9, 1970<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> and it began operation on December 2, 1970, after Nixon signed an <a href="/wiki/Executive_order" title="Executive order">executive order</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Reorganization_Plan_No._3" title="Reorganization Plan No. 3">order establishing the EPA</a> was ratified by committee hearings in the House and Senate. The agency is led by its <a href="/wiki/Administrator_of_the_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="Administrator of the Environmental Protection Agency">Administrator</a>, who is appointed by the <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President</a> and approved by Congress. The current acting Administrator following the resignation of <a href="/wiki/Scott_Pruitt" title="Scott Pruitt">Scott Pruitt</a> is  Deputy Administrator <a href="/wiki/Andrew_R._Wheeler" title="Andrew R. Wheeler">Andrew Wheeler</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> The EPA is not a <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_the_United_States" title="Cabinet of the United States">Cabinet</a> department, but the Administrator is normally given <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_the_United_States#Cabinet-level_officials" title="Cabinet of the United States">cabinet rank</a>.
</p><p>The EPA has its headquarters in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a>, regional offices for each of the agency's ten <a href="#EPA_regions">regions</a>, and 27 laboratories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> The agency conducts <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_assessment" title="Environmental assessment">environmental assessment</a>, research, and education. It has the responsibility of maintaining and enforcing national standards under a variety of environmental laws, in consultation with state, tribal, and local governments. It delegates some permitting, monitoring, and enforcement responsibility to <a href="/wiki/U.S._state" title="U.S. state">U.S. states</a> and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_federally_recognized_tribes" title="List of federally recognized tribes">federally recognized tribes</a>. EPA enforcement powers include fines, <a href="/wiki/Sanctions_(law)" title="Sanctions (law)">sanctions</a>, and other measures. The agency also works with industries and all levels of government in a wide variety of voluntary pollution prevention programs and energy conservation efforts.
</p><p>In 2018, the agency had 14,172 full-time employees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPA's_Budget_and_Spending_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-EPA's_Budget_and_Spending-7">[7]</a></sup> More than half of EPA's employees are engineers, scientists, and environmental protection specialists; other employees include legal, public affairs, financial, and information technologists. In 2017 the Trump administration proposed a 31% cut to the EPA's budget to $5.7 billion from $8.1 billion and to eliminate a quarter of the agency jobs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> However, this cut was not approved by Congress.
</p><p>The Environmental Protection Agency can only act under statutes, which are the authority of laws passed by Congress. Congress must approve the statute and they also have the power to authorize or prohibit certain actions, which the EPA has to implement and enforce. Appropriations statutes authorize how much money the agency can spend each year to carry out the approved statutes. The Environmental Protection Agency has the power to issue regulations. A regulation is a standard or rule written by the agency to interpret the statute, apply it in situations and enforce it. Congress allows the EPA to write regulations in order to solve a problem, but the agency must include a rationale of why the regulations need to be implemented. The regulations can be challenged by the Courts, where the regulation is overruled or confirmed. Many public health and environmental groups advocate for the agency and believe that it is creating a better world. Other critics believe that the agency commits government overreach by adding unnecessary regulations on business and property owners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BURNING_DISCARDED_AUTOMOBILE_BATTERIES._(FROM_THE_SITES_EXHIBITION._FOR_OTHER_IMAGES_IN_THIS_ASSIGNMENT,_SEE_FICHE..._-_NARA_-_553841.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2034" data-file-width="3000" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/BURNING_DISCARDED_AUTOMOBILE_BATTERIES._%28FROM_THE_SITES_EXHIBITION._FOR_OTHER_IMAGES_IN_THIS_ASSIGNMENT%2C_SEE_FICHE..._-_NARA_-_553841.jpg/220px-BURNING_DISCARDED_AUTOMOBILE_BATTERIES._%28FROM_THE_SITES_EXHIBITION._FOR_OTHER_IMAGES_IN_THIS_ASSIGNMENT%2C_SEE_FICHE..._-_NARA_-_553841.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/BURNING_DISCARDED_AUTOMOBILE_BATTERIES._%28FROM_THE_SITES_EXHIBITION._FOR_OTHER_IMAGES_IN_THIS_ASSIGNMENT%2C_SEE_FICHE..._-_NARA_-_553841.jpg/330px-BURNING_DISCARDED_AUTOMOBILE_BATTERIES._%28FROM_THE_SITES_EXHIBITION._FOR_OTHER_IMAGES_IN_THIS_ASSIGNMENT%2C_SEE_FICHE..._-_NARA_-_553841.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/BURNING_DISCARDED_AUTOMOBILE_BATTERIES._%28FROM_THE_SITES_EXHIBITION._FOR_OTHER_IMAGES_IN_THIS_ASSIGNMENT%2C_SEE_FICHE..._-_NARA_-_553841.jpg/440px-BURNING_DISCARDED_AUTOMOBILE_BATTERIES._%28FROM_THE_SITES_EXHIBITION._FOR_OTHER_IMAGES_IN_THIS_ASSIGNMENT%2C_SEE_FICHE..._-_NARA_-_553841.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BURNING_DISCARDED_AUTOMOBILE_BATTERIES._(FROM_THE_SITES_EXHIBITION._FOR_OTHER_IMAGES_IN_THIS_ASSIGNMENT,_SEE_FICHE..._-_NARA_-_553841.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Stacks emitting smoke from burning discarded automobile batteries, photo taken in Houston in 1972 by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Marc_St._Gil&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Marc St. Gil (page does not exist)">Marc St. Gil</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> (<a class="extiw" href="https://cs.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marc_St._Gil" title="cs:Marc St. Gil">cs</a>)</span>, official photographer of recently founded EPA</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SMOKESTACKS_OF_THE_HOLMES_ROAD_INCINERATOR,_NO_LONGER_POLLUTE_THE_ATMOSPHERE._THE_PLANT_PREVIOUSLY_BURNED_OLD..._-_NARA_-_557411.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2026" height="326" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/SMOKESTACKS_OF_THE_HOLMES_ROAD_INCINERATOR%2C_NO_LONGER_POLLUTE_THE_ATMOSPHERE._THE_PLANT_PREVIOUSLY_BURNED_OLD..._-_NARA_-_557411.jpg/220px-SMOKESTACKS_OF_THE_HOLMES_ROAD_INCINERATOR%2C_NO_LONGER_POLLUTE_THE_ATMOSPHERE._THE_PLANT_PREVIOUSLY_BURNED_OLD..._-_NARA_-_557411.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/SMOKESTACKS_OF_THE_HOLMES_ROAD_INCINERATOR%2C_NO_LONGER_POLLUTE_THE_ATMOSPHERE._THE_PLANT_PREVIOUSLY_BURNED_OLD..._-_NARA_-_557411.jpg/330px-SMOKESTACKS_OF_THE_HOLMES_ROAD_INCINERATOR%2C_NO_LONGER_POLLUTE_THE_ATMOSPHERE._THE_PLANT_PREVIOUSLY_BURNED_OLD..._-_NARA_-_557411.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/SMOKESTACKS_OF_THE_HOLMES_ROAD_INCINERATOR%2C_NO_LONGER_POLLUTE_THE_ATMOSPHERE._THE_PLANT_PREVIOUSLY_BURNED_OLD..._-_NARA_-_557411.jpg/440px-SMOKESTACKS_OF_THE_HOLMES_ROAD_INCINERATOR%2C_NO_LONGER_POLLUTE_THE_ATMOSPHERE._THE_PLANT_PREVIOUSLY_BURNED_OLD..._-_NARA_-_557411.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SMOKESTACKS_OF_THE_HOLMES_ROAD_INCINERATOR,_NO_LONGER_POLLUTE_THE_ATMOSPHERE._THE_PLANT_PREVIOUSLY_BURNED_OLD..._-_NARA_-_557411.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Same smokestacks in 1975 after the plant was closed in a push for greater environmental protection</div></div></div>
<p>Beginning in the late 1950s and through the 1960s, Congress reacted to increasing public concern about the impact that human activity could have on the environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Distillations_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Distillations-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPA-guardian_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-EPA-guardian-12">[12]</a></sup> Senator <a href="/wiki/James_E._Murray" title="James E. Murray">James E. Murray</a> introduced a bill, the Resources and Conservation Act (RCA) of 1959, in the <a href="/wiki/86th_United_States_Congress" title="86th United States Congress">86th Congress</a>. The 1962 publication of <i><a href="/wiki/Silent_Spring" title="Silent Spring">Silent Spring</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Rachel_Carson" title="Rachel Carson">Rachel Carson</a> alerted the public about the detrimental effects on the environment of the indiscriminate use of <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the years following, similar bills were introduced and hearings were held to discuss the state of the environment and Congress's potential responses. In 1968, a joint House–Senate colloquium was convened by the chairmen of the Senate Committee on Interior and Insular Affairs, Senator <a href="/wiki/Henry_M._Jackson" title="Henry M. Jackson">Henry M. Jackson</a>, and the House Committee on Science and Astronautics, Representative <a href="/wiki/George_Miller_(California_politician)" title="George Miller (California politician)">George Miller</a>, to discuss the need for and means of implementing a national environmental policy. In the colloquium, some members of Congress expressed a continuing concern over federal agency actions affecting the environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/National_Environmental_Policy_Act" title="National Environmental Policy Act">National Environmental Policy Act of 1969</a> (NEPA)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> was modeled on the Resources and Conservation Act of 1959 (RCA).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> RCA would have established a Council on Environmental Quality in the office of the President, declared a national environmental policy, and required the preparation of an annual environmental report.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Luther_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Luther-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Caldwell_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Caldwell-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>President Nixon signed NEPA into law on January 1, 1970. The law created the <a href="/wiki/Council_on_Environmental_Quality" title="Council on Environmental Quality">Council on Environmental Quality</a> (CEQ) in the <a href="/wiki/Executive_Office_of_the_President_of_the_United_States" title="Executive Office of the President of the United States">Executive Office of the President</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Distillations_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Distillations-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> NEPA required that a detailed statement of environmental impacts be prepared for all major federal actions significantly affecting the environment. The "detailed statement" would ultimately be referred to as an <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_statement" title="Environmental impact statement">environmental impact statement</a> (EIS).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Distillations_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-Distillations-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:William_Ruckelshaus_Swearing_In_as_EPA_Administrator.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="632" data-file-width="981" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/William_Ruckelshaus_Swearing_In_as_EPA_Administrator.jpg/220px-William_Ruckelshaus_Swearing_In_as_EPA_Administrator.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/William_Ruckelshaus_Swearing_In_as_EPA_Administrator.jpg/330px-William_Ruckelshaus_Swearing_In_as_EPA_Administrator.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/William_Ruckelshaus_Swearing_In_as_EPA_Administrator.jpg/440px-William_Ruckelshaus_Swearing_In_as_EPA_Administrator.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:William_Ruckelshaus_Swearing_In_as_EPA_Administrator.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ruckelshaus sworn in as first EPA Administrator.</div></div></div>
<p>On July 9, 1970, Nixon proposed an <a href="/wiki/Reorganization_Plan_No._3" title="Reorganization Plan No. 3">executive reorganization</a> that consolidated many environmental responsibilities of the federal government under one agency, a new Environmental Protection Agency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> This proposal included merging antipollution programs from a number of departments, such as the combination of pesticide programs from the United States Department of Agriculture, Department of Interior, and U.S. Department of Interior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-23">[23]</a></sup> After conducting hearings during that summer, the House and Senate approved the proposal. The EPA was created 90 days before it had to operate<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>, and officially opened its doors on December 2, 1970. The agency's first Administrator, <a href="/wiki/William_Ruckelshaus" title="William Ruckelshaus">William Ruckelshaus</a>, took the oath of office on December 4, 1970.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPA-guardian_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-EPA-guardian-12">[12]</a></sup>  In its first year, the EPA had a budget of $1.4 billion and 5,800 employees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-23">[23]</a></sup> At its start, the EPA was primarily a technical assistance agency that set goals and standards. Soon, new acts and amendments passed by Congress gave the agency its regulatory authority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>EPA staff recall that in the early days there was "an enormous sense of purpose and excitement" and the expectation that "there was this agency which was going to do something about a problem that clearly was on the minds of a lot of people in this country," leading to tens of thousands of resumes from those eager to participate in the mighty effort to clean up America's environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>When EPA first began operation, members of the private sector felt strongly that the environmental protection movement was a passing fad. Ruckelshaus stated that he felt pressure to show a public which was deeply skeptical about government's effectiveness, that EPA could respond effectively to widespread concerns about pollution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>In April 1986, when the <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_disaster" title="Chernobyl disaster">Chernobyl</a> disaster occurred, the EPA was tasked with identifying an impacts on the United States and keeping the public informed. Administrator Lee Thomas assembled a cross-agency team, including personal from the Nuclear Regulatory Commission, National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration, and the Department of Energy to monitor the situation. They held press conferences for 10 days<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>. 
</p><p>The EPA also researched the implications of stratospheric ozone depletion. Under the leadership of Administrator Lee Thomas, the EPA joined with several international organizations to perform a risk assessment of stratospheric ozone<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>, which helped provide motivation for the Montreal Protocol, which was agreed to in August 1987. 
</p><p>In 1988, during his first presidential campaign, <a href="/wiki/George_H._W._Bush" title="George H. W. Bush">George H. W. Bush</a> was vocal about environmental issues. He appointed as his EPA administrator William Reilly, an environmentalist. Under Reilly’s leadership, the EPA implemented voluntary programs and a cluster rule for multimedia regulation. At time, the environment was increasingly being recognized as a regional issue, which was reflected in 1990 amendment of the Clean Air Act and new approaches by the agency<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Organization">Organization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Organization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The EPA is led by an <a href="/wiki/Administrator_of_the_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="Administrator of the Environmental Protection Agency">Administrator of the Environmental Protection Agency</a>. From February 2017 to July 2018, <a href="/wiki/Scott_Pruitt" title="Scott Pruitt">Scott Pruitt</a> served as the 14th Administrator. The current acting administrator is Deputy Administrator <a href="/wiki/Andrew_R._Wheeler" title="Andrew R. Wheeler">Andrew R. Wheeler</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Offices">Offices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Offices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>Office of the Administrator (OA).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> As of March 2017 the office consisted of 11 divisions, the Office of Administrative and Executive Services, Office of Children's Health Protection, Office of Civil Rights, Office of Congressional and Intergovernmental Relations, Office of the Executive Secretariat, Office of Homeland Security, Office of Policy, Office of Public Affairs, Office of Public Engagement and Environmental Education, Office of Small and Disadvantaged Business Utilization, Science Advisory Board.</li>
<li>Office of Administration and Resources Management (OARM)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></li>
<li>Office of Air and Radiation (OAR)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></li>
<li>Office of Chemical Safety and Pollution Prevention (OCSPP)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></li>
<li>Office of the Chief Financial Officer (OCFO)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Office_of_Enforcement_and_Compliance_Assurance" title="Office of Enforcement and Compliance Assurance">Office of Enforcement and Compliance Assurance</a> (OECA)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></li>
<li>Office of Environmental Information (OEI)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></li>
<li>Office of General Counsel (OGC)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></li>
<li>Office of <a href="/wiki/Inspector_general#United_States" title="Inspector general">Inspector General</a> (OIG)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></li>
<li>Office of International and Tribal Affairs (OITA)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></li>
<li>Office of Research and Development (ORD)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> which as of March 2017 consisted of the</li></ul>
<dl><dd><dl><dd>National Center for Computational Toxicology, National Center for Environmental Assessment,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> National Center for Environmental Research, National Exposure Research Laboratory, National Health and Environmental Effects Research Laboratory, National Homeland Security Research Center, National Risk Management Research Laboratory</dd></dl></dd></dl>
<ul><li>Office of Land and Emergency Management (OLEM)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></li></ul>
<dl><dd><dl><dd>which as of March 2017 consisted of the Office of Superfund Remediation and Technology Innovation, Office of Resource Conservation and Recovery, Office of Underground Storage Tanks, Office of Brownfields and Land Revitalization, Office of Emergency Management, Federal Facilities Restoration and Reuse Office.</dd></dl></dd></dl>
<ul><li>Office of Water (OW)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> which as of March 2017 consisted of the Office of Ground Water and Drinking Water (OGWDW), Office of Science and Technology (OST), Office of Wastewater Management (OWM) and Office of Wetlands, Oceans and Watersheds (OWOW).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regions">Regions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Regions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Regions_of_the_United_States_EPA.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="593" data-file-width="959" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Regions_of_the_United_States_EPA.svg/220px-Regions_of_the_United_States_EPA.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Regions_of_the_United_States_EPA.svg/330px-Regions_of_the_United_States_EPA.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Regions_of_the_United_States_EPA.svg/440px-Regions_of_the_United_States_EPA.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Regions_of_the_United_States_EPA.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The administrative regions of the United States Environmental Protection Agency.</div></div></div>
<p>Creating 10 EPA regions was an initiative that came from President Richard Nixon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> <i>See</i> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_Federal_Regions" title="Standard Federal Regions">Standard Federal Regions</a>.
</p><p>Each EPA regional office is responsible within its states for implementing the Agency's programs, except those programs that have been specifically delegated to states.
</p>
<ul><li>Region 1: responsible within the states of <a href="/wiki/Connecticut" title="Connecticut">Connecticut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maine" title="Maine">Maine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire" title="New Hampshire">New Hampshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhode_Island" title="Rhode Island">Rhode Island</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vermont" title="Vermont">Vermont</a> (<a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a>).</li>
<li>Region 2: responsible within the states of <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a>. It is also responsible for the US territories of <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Virgin_Islands" title="U.S. Virgin Islands">U.S. Virgin Islands</a>.</li>
<li>Region 3: responsible within the states of <a href="/wiki/Delaware" title="Delaware">Delaware</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>, <a href="/wiki/West_Virginia" title="West Virginia">West Virginia</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia" title="District of Columbia">District of Columbia</a>.</li>
<li>Region 4: responsible within the states of <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kentucky" title="Kentucky">Kentucky</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North Carolina</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tennessee" title="Tennessee">Tennessee</a>.</li>
<li>Region 5: responsible within the states of <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indiana" title="Indiana">Indiana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a>.</li>
<li>Region 6: responsible within the states of <a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">Arkansas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma" title="Oklahoma">Oklahoma</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>.</li>
<li>Region 7: responsible within the states of <a href="/wiki/Iowa" title="Iowa">Iowa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kansas" title="Kansas">Kansas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Missouri" title="Missouri">Missouri</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nebraska" title="Nebraska">Nebraska</a>.</li>
<li>Region 8: responsible within the states of <a href="/wiki/Colorado" title="Colorado">Colorado</a>, <a href="/wiki/Montana" title="Montana">Montana</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Dakota" title="North Dakota">North Dakota</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Dakota" title="South Dakota">South Dakota</a>, <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wyoming" title="Wyoming">Wyoming</a>.</li>
<li>Region 9: responsible within the states of <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a>, <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nevada" title="Nevada">Nevada</a>, the territories of <a href="/wiki/Guam" title="Guam">Guam</a> and <a href="/wiki/American_Samoa" title="American Samoa">American Samoa</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Navajo_Nation" title="Navajo Nation">Navajo Nation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></li>
<li>Region 10: responsible within the states of <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>, <a href="/wiki/Idaho" title="Idaho">Idaho</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oregon" title="Oregon">Oregon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Washington_(state)" title="Washington (state)">Washington</a>.</li></ul>
<p>Each regional office also implements programs on <a href="/wiki/List_of_Indian_reservations_in_the_United_States" title="List of Indian reservations in the United States">Indian Tribal lands</a>, except those programs delegated to tribal authorities.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Related_legislation">Related legislation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Related legislation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>EPA has principal implementation authority for the following federal environmental laws:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Clean_Air_Act_(United_States)" title="Clean Air Act (United States)">Clean Air Act</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Clean_Water_Act" title="Clean Water Act">Clean Water Act</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comprehensive_Environmental_Response,_Compensation_and_Liability_Act" title="Comprehensive Environmental Response, Compensation and Liability Act">Comprehensive Environmental Response, Compensation and Liability Act</a> ("Superfund")</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Emergency_Planning_and_Community_Right-to-Know_Act" title="Emergency Planning and Community Right-to-Know Act">Emergency Planning and Community Right-to-Know Act</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Federal_Insecticide,_Fungicide,_and_Rodenticide_Act" title="Federal Insecticide, Fungicide, and Rodenticide Act">Federal Insecticide, Fungicide, and Rodenticide Act</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Resource_Conservation_and_Recovery_Act" title="Resource Conservation and Recovery Act">Resource Conservation and Recovery Act</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Safe_Drinking_Water_Act" title="Safe Drinking Water Act">Safe Drinking Water Act</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toxic_Substances_Control_Act" title="Toxic Substances Control Act">Toxic Substances Control Act</a>.</li></ul>
<p>There are additional laws where EPA has a contributing role or provides assistance to other agencies. Among these laws are:
</p>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endangered_Species_Act" title="Endangered Species Act">Endangered Species Act</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energy_Independence_and_Security_Act" title="Energy Independence and Security Act">Energy Independence and Security Act</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Energy_Policy_Act_of_2005" title="Energy Policy Act of 2005">Energy Policy Act</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Federal_Food,_Drug,_and_Cosmetic_Act" title="Federal Food, Drug, and Cosmetic Act">Federal Food, Drug, and Cosmetic Act</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Food_Quality_Protection_Act" title="Food Quality Protection Act">Food Quality Protection Act</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/National_Environmental_Policy_Act" title="National Environmental Policy Act">National Environmental Policy Act</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_Pollution_Act" title="Oil Pollution Act">Oil Pollution Act</a></li>
<li>Pollution Prevention Act.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Programs">Programs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Programs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_BULLDOZER_PILES_BOULDERS_IN_AN_ATTEMPT_TO_PREVENT_LAKE_SHORE_EROSION_-_NARA_-_547031.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2080" data-file-width="3000" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/A_BULLDOZER_PILES_BOULDERS_IN_AN_ATTEMPT_TO_PREVENT_LAKE_SHORE_EROSION_-_NARA_-_547031.jpg/220px-A_BULLDOZER_PILES_BOULDERS_IN_AN_ATTEMPT_TO_PREVENT_LAKE_SHORE_EROSION_-_NARA_-_547031.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/A_BULLDOZER_PILES_BOULDERS_IN_AN_ATTEMPT_TO_PREVENT_LAKE_SHORE_EROSION_-_NARA_-_547031.jpg/330px-A_BULLDOZER_PILES_BOULDERS_IN_AN_ATTEMPT_TO_PREVENT_LAKE_SHORE_EROSION_-_NARA_-_547031.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/A_BULLDOZER_PILES_BOULDERS_IN_AN_ATTEMPT_TO_PREVENT_LAKE_SHORE_EROSION_-_NARA_-_547031.jpg/440px-A_BULLDOZER_PILES_BOULDERS_IN_AN_ATTEMPT_TO_PREVENT_LAKE_SHORE_EROSION_-_NARA_-_547031.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_BULLDOZER_PILES_BOULDERS_IN_AN_ATTEMPT_TO_PREVENT_LAKE_SHORE_EROSION_-_NARA_-_547031.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A bulldozer piles boulders in an attempt to prevent lake shore erosion, 1973 (photograph by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Paul_Sequeira&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Paul Sequeira (page does not exist)">Paul Sequeira</a>, photojournalist and contributing photographer to the Environmental Protection Agency's <a href="/wiki/DOCUMERICA" title="DOCUMERICA">DOCUMERICA</a> project in the early 1970s)</div></div></div>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w.svg"><img alt="Wiki letter w.svg" data-file-height="44" data-file-width="44" height="44" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/6c/Wiki_letter_w.svg/44px-Wiki_letter_w.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/6c/Wiki_letter_w.svg/66px-Wiki_letter_w.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/6c/Wiki_letter_w.svg/88px-Wiki_letter_w.svg.png 2x" width="44"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>is missing information about several major EPA programs</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please expand the section to include this information. Further details may exist on the <a href="/wiki/Talk:United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="Talk:United States Environmental Protection Agency">talk page</a>.</span> <small><i>(October 2010)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>It is worth noting that, in looking back in 2013 on the agency he helped shape from the beginning, Administrator William Ruckelshaus observed that a danger for EPA was that air, water, waste and other programs would be unconnected, placed in "silos," a problem that persists more than 50 years later, albeit less so than at the start.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="EPA_Safer_Choice">EPA Safer Choice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: EPA Safer Choice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/EPA_Safer_Choice" title="EPA Safer Choice">EPA Safer Choice</a> label, previously known as the Design for the Environment (DfE) label, helps consumers and commercial buyers identify and select products with safer chemical ingredients, without sacrificing quality or performance. When a product has the Safer Choice label, it means that every intentionally-added ingredient in the product has been evaluated by EPA scientists. Only the safest possible functional ingredients are allowed in products with the Safer Choice label.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Safer_Detergents_Stewardship_Initiative">Safer Detergents Stewardship Initiative</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Safer Detergents Stewardship Initiative">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EPA_Safer_Detergents_Stewardship_Initiative" title="EPA Safer Detergents Stewardship Initiative">Safer Detergents Stewardship Initiative</a> (SDSI),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sdsi_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-sdsi-49">[49]</a></sup> EPA's Design for the Environment (DfE) recognizes environmental leaders who voluntarily commit to the use of safer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surfactants" title="Surfactants">surfactants</a>. Safer surfactants are the ones that break down quickly to non-polluting compounds and help protect aquatic life in both fresh and salt water. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nonylphenol_ethoxylates" title="Nonylphenol ethoxylates">Nonylphenol ethoxylates</a>, commonly referred to as NPEs, are an example of a surfactant class that does not meet the definition of a safer surfactant.
</p><p>The Design for the Environment, which was renamed to <a href="/wiki/EPA_Safer_Choice" title="EPA Safer Choice">EPA Safer Choice</a> in 2015, has identified safer alternative surfactants through partnerships with industry and environmental advocates. These safer alternatives are comparable in cost and are readily available. CleanGredients<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cleangredients_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-cleangredients-50">[50]</a></sup> is a source of safer surfactants.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_Star">Energy Star</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Energy Star">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1992 the EPA launched the <a href="/wiki/Energy_Star" title="Energy Star">Energy Star</a> program, a voluntary program that fosters energy efficiency. This program came out an increased effort to collaborate with industry. At the start, it motivated major companies to retrofit millions of square feet of building space with more efficient lighting<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>. As of 2006, more than 40,000 Energy Star products were available including major appliances, office equipment, lighting, home electronics, and more. In addition, the label can also be found on new homes and commercial and industrial buildings. In 2006, about 12 percent of new housing in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> was labeled Energy Star.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPA2006_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-EPA2006-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>The EPA estimates it saved about $14 billion in energy costs in 2006 alone. The Energy Star program has helped spread the use of <a href="/wiki/Light-emitting_diode" title="Light-emitting diode">LED</a> <a href="/wiki/Traffic_light" title="Traffic light">traffic lights</a>, efficient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fluorescent_light" title="Fluorescent light">fluorescent lighting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Power_management" title="Power management">power management</a> systems for office equipment, and low <a href="/wiki/Standby_power" title="Standby power">standby</a> energy use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Smart_Growth">Smart Growth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Smart Growth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>EPA's Smart Growth Program, which began in 1998, is to help communities improve their development practices and get the type of development they want. Together with local, state, and national experts, EPA encourages development strategies that protect human health and the environment, create economic opportunities, and provide attractive and affordable neighborhoods for people of all income levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epa.gov_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-epa.gov-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pesticides">Pesticides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Pesticides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>EPA administers the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Insecticide,_Fungicide,_and_Rodenticide_Act" title="Federal Insecticide, Fungicide, and Rodenticide Act">Federal Insecticide, Fungicide, and Rodenticide Act</a> (FIFRA) (which is much older than the agency) and registers all <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a> legally sold in the United States.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fuel_economy">Fuel economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Fuel economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The testing system was originally developed in 1972 and used <a href="/wiki/Driving_cycle" title="Driving cycle">driving cycles</a> designed to simulate driving during rush-hour in <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a> during that era. Until 1984 the EPA reported the exact fuel economy figures calculated from the test.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In 1984, the EPA began adjusting city (aka <a href="/wiki/UDDS" title="UDDS">Urban Dynamometer Driving Schedule</a> or <a href="/wiki/UDDS" title="UDDS">UDDS</a>) results downward by 10% and highway (aka HighWay Fuel Economy Test or HWFET) results by 22% to compensate for changes in driving conditions since 1972, and to better correlate the EPA test results with real-world driving. In 1996, the EPA proposed updating the Federal Testing Procedures<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> to add a new higher-speed test (US06) and an air-conditioner-on test (SC03) to further improve the correlation of fuel economy and emission estimates with real-world reports. In December 2006 the updated testing methodology was finalized to be implemented in model year 2008 vehicles and set the precedent of a 12-year review cycle for the test procedures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epafe_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-epafe-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>In February 2005, EPA launched a program called "Your MPG" that allows drivers to add real-world fuel economy statistics into a database on the EPA's fuel economy website and compare them with others and with the original EPA test results.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>The EPA conducts fuel economy tests on very few vehicles. "Just 18 of the EPA's 17,000 employees work in the automobile-testing department in Ann Arbor, Michigan, examining 200 to 250 vehicles a year, or roughly 15 percent of new models. As to that other 85 percent, the EPA takes automakers at their word—without any testing-accepting submitted results as accurate."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Two-thirds of the vehicles the EPA tests themselves are randomly selected and the remaining third is tested for specific reasons.
</p><p>Although originally created as a reference point for fossil-fueled vehicles, <a href="/wiki/Driving_cycle" title="Driving cycle">driving cycles</a> have been used for estimating how many miles an electric vehicle will get on a single charge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oil_spill_prevention_program">Oil spill prevention program</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Oil spill prevention program">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>EPA's oil spill prevention program includes the Spill Prevention, Control, and Countermeasure (SPCC) and the Facility Response Plan (FRP) rules. The SPCC Rule applies to all facilities that store, handle, process, gather, transfer, refine, distribute, use or consume oil or oil products. Oil products includes petroleum and non-petroleum oils as well as: animal fats, oils and greases; fish and marine mammal oils; and vegetable oils. It mandates a written plan for facilities that store more than 1,320 gallons of fuel above ground or more than 42,000 gallons below-ground, and which might discharge to navigable waters (as defined in the <a href="/wiki/Clean_Water_Act" title="Clean Water Act">Clean Water Act</a>) or adjoining shorelines. <a href="/wiki/Secondary_spill_containment" title="Secondary spill containment">Secondary spill containment</a> is mandated at oil storage facilities and oil release containment is required at oil development sites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Toxics_Release_Inventory">Toxics Release Inventory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Toxics Release Inventory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Toxics_Release_Inventory" title="Toxics Release Inventory">Toxics Release Inventory</a> (TRI) is a resource for learning about toxic chemical releases and pollution prevention activities reported by industrial and federal facilities. TRI data support informed decision-making by communities, government agencies, companies, and others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="WaterSense">WaterSense</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: WaterSense">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WaterSense" title="WaterSense">WaterSense</a> is an EPA program launched in June 2006 to encourage <a href="/wiki/Water_efficiency" title="Water efficiency">water efficiency</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> through the use of a special <a href="/wiki/Label" title="Label">label</a> on <a href="/wiki/Consumer" title="Consumer">consumer</a> <a href="/wiki/Product_(business)" title="Product (business)">products</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Timeline_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Timeline-62">[62]</a></sup> Products include high-efficiency toilets (HETs), bathroom sink faucets (and accessories), and irrigation equipment. WaterSense is a <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/voluntary" title="wikt:voluntary">voluntary</a> program, with EPA developing specifications for water-efficient products through a public process and product testing by independent laboratories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Underground_Storage_Tanks_Program">Underground Storage Tanks Program</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Underground Storage Tanks Program">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>EPA regulates underground storage tanks (USTs) containing petroleum and hazardous chemicals under Subtitle I of the <a href="/wiki/Resource_Conservation_and_Recovery_Act" title="Resource Conservation and Recovery Act">Solid Waste Disposal Act</a>. This program was launched in 1985 and covers about 553,000 active USTs. Since 1984, 1.8 million USTs have been closed in compliance with regulations. 38 states, the District of Columbia and Puerto Rico manage UST programs with EPA authorization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> When the program began EPA had only 90 staff to develop a system to regulate more than 2 million tanks and work with 750,000 owners and operators. Administrator Lee Thomas told the program’s new manager, Ron Brand, that it would have to be done differently that EPA’s traditional approach. This program therefore behaves differently than other EPA offices, focusing much more on local operations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Drinking_water">Drinking water</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Drinking water">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>EPA ensures safe <a href="/wiki/Drinking_water" title="Drinking water">drinking water</a> for the public, by setting standards for more than 160,000 <a href="/wiki/Public_water_system" title="Public water system">public water systems</a> nationwide. EPA oversees states, local governments and water suppliers to enforce the standards under the <a href="/wiki/Safe_Drinking_Water_Act" title="Safe Drinking Water Act">Safe Drinking Water Act</a>. The program includes regulation of <a href="/wiki/Injection_well" title="Injection well">injection wells</a> in order to protect underground sources of drinking water. Select readings of amounts of certain contaminants in drinking water, precipitation, and surface water, in addition to milk and air, are reported on EPA's Rad Net web site<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> in a section entitled Envirofacts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
Despite mandatory reporting certain readings exceeding EPA MCL levels may be deleted or not included.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bauer2012B_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bauer2012B-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RadNet2_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-RadNet2-69">[69]</a></sup> In 2013, an EPA draft revision relaxed regulations for radiation exposure through drinking water, stating that current standards are impractical to enforce. The EPA recommended that intervention was not necessary until drinking water was contaminated with radioactive iodine 131 at a concentration of 81,000 picocuries per liter (the limit for short term exposure set by the International Atomic Energy Agency), which was 27,000 times the prior EPA limit of 3 picocuries per liter for long term exposure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPA_relaxes_regulations_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-EPA_relaxes_regulations-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="National_Pollutant_Discharge_Elimination_System">National Pollutant Discharge Elimination System</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: National Pollutant Discharge Elimination System">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/United_States_regulation_of_point_source_water_pollution" title="United States regulation of point source water pollution">United States regulation of point source water pollution</a></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Pollutant_Discharge_Elimination_System" title="National Pollutant Discharge Elimination System">National Pollutant Discharge Elimination System</a> (NPDES) permit program addresses <a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">water pollution</a> by regulating <a href="/wiki/Point_source" title="Point source">point sources</a> which discharge to US waters. Created in 1972 by the Clean Water Act, the NPDES permit program authorizes state governments to perform its many permitting, administrative, and enforcement aspects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-About_NPDES_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-About_NPDES-71">[71]</a></sup> As of 2017, EPA has approved 46 states to administer all or portions of the permit program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> EPA regional offices manage the program in the remaining areas of the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-About_NPDES_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-About_NPDES-71">[71]</a></sup> The Water Quality Act of 1987 extended NPDES permit coverage to <a href="/wiki/Industrial_stormwater" title="Industrial stormwater">industrial stormwater</a> dischargers and municipal separate storm sewer systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radiation_protection">Radiation protection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Radiation protection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>EPA has the following seven project groups to protect the public from radiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>Radioactive Waste Management<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></li>
<li>Emergency Preparedness and Response Programs<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Protective Action Guides And Planning Guidance for Radiological Incidents: EPA developed a manual as guideline for local and state governments to protect the public from a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_accident" title="Nuclear accident">nuclear accident</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> the 2017 version being a 15-year update.</li>
<li>EPA's Role in Emergency Response – Special Teams<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></li>
<li>Technologically Enhanced Naturally Occurring Radioactive Materials (TENORM) Program<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></li>
<li>Radiation Standards for Air and Drinking Water Programs<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></li>
<li>Federal Guidance for Radiation Protection<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></li></ol>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tools_for_Schools">Tools for Schools</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Tools for Schools">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>EPA's Indoor Air Quality Tools for Schools Program helps schools to maintain a healthy environment and reduce exposures to indoor environmental contaminants. It helps school personnel identify, solve, and prevent indoor air quality problems in the school environment. Through the use of a multi-step management plan and checklists for the entire building, schools can lower their students' and staff's risk of exposure to asthma triggers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_education">Environmental education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Environmental education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/National_Environmental_Education_Act" title="National Environmental Education Act">National Environmental Education Act</a> of 1990 requires EPA to provide national leadership to increase environmental literacy. EPA established the Office of Environmental Education to implement this program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Clean_School_Bus_USA">Clean School Bus USA</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Clean School Bus USA">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Clean School Bus USA is a national partnership to reduce children's exposure to diesel exhaust by eliminating unnecessary school bus idling, installing effective emission control systems on newer buses and replacing the oldest buses in the fleet with newer ones. Its goal is to reduce both children's exposure to diesel exhaust and the amount of air pollution created by diesel school buses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="33.2F50_Program_.28Past.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="33/50_Program_(Past)">33/50 Program (Past)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: 33/50 Program (Past)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1991 under Administrator William Reilly, the EPA implemented its voluntary 33/50 program<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>. This was designed to encourage, recognize, and celebrate companies that voluntarily found ways to prevent and reduce pollution in their operations<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_justice">Environmental justice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Environmental justice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The EPA has been criticized for its lack of progress towards <a href="/wiki/Environmental_justice" title="Environmental justice">environmental justice</a>. Administrator <a href="/wiki/Christine_Todd_Whitman" title="Christine Todd Whitman">Christine Todd Whitman</a> was criticized for her changes to President Bill Clinton's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Executive_order_(United_States)" title="Executive order (United States)">Executive Order</a> 12898 during 2001, removing the requirements for government agencies to take the poor and minority populations into special consideration when making changes to environmental legislation, and therefore defeating the spirit of the Executive Order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O'Neil_report_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-O'Neil_report-87">[87]</a></sup> In a March 2004 report, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Office_of_the_Inspector_General" title="Office of the Inspector General">inspector general</a> of the agency concluded that the EPA "has not developed a clear vision or a comprehensive strategic plan, and has not established values, goals, expectations, and performance measurements" for environmental justice in its daily operations. Another report in September 2006 found the agency still had failed to review the success of its programs, policies and activities towards environmental justice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Studies have also found that poor and minority populations were underserved by the EPA's <a href="/wiki/Superfund" title="Superfund">Superfund</a> program, and that this situation was worsening.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O'Neil_report_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-O'Neil_report-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Barriers_to_enforcing_environmental_justice">Barriers to enforcing environmental justice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Barriers to enforcing environmental justice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Original_research" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="" data-file-height="40" data-file-width="40" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/40px-Ambox_important.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/60px-Ambox_important.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/80px-Ambox_important.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section  <b>possibly contains <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research">original research</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit">improve it</a> by <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verifying</a> the claims made and adding <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#Inline_citations" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">inline citations</a>. Statements consisting only of original research should be removed.</span> <small><i>(March 2017)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Many environmental justice issues are local, and therefore difficult to address by a federal agency, such as the EPA. Without strong media attention, political interest, or 'crisis' status, local issues are less likely to be addressed at the federal level compared to larger, well publicized incidents.
</p><p>Conflicting political powers in successive administrations: The White House maintains direct control over the EPA, and its enforcements are subject to the political agenda of who is in power. Republicans and Democrats differ in their approaches to environmental justice. While President Bill Clinton signed the executive order 12898, the Bush administration did not develop a clear plan or establish goals for integrating environmental justice into everyday practices, affecting the motivation for environmental enforcement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (March 2017)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The EPA is responsible for preventing and detecting environmental crimes, informing the public of environmental enforcement, and setting and monitoring standards of air pollution, water pollution, hazardous wastes and chemicals. "It is difficult to construct a specific mission statement given its wide range of responsibilities."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (March 2017)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> It is impossible to address every environmental crime adequately or efficiently if there is no specific mission statement to refer to. The EPA answers to various groups, competes for resources, and confronts a wide array of harms to the environment. All of these present challenges, including a lack of resources, its self-policing policy, and a broadly defined legislation that creates too much discretion for EPA officers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (March 2017)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The EPA "does not have the authority or resources to address injustices without an increase in federal mandates" requiring private industries to consider the environmental ramifications of their activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Research_vessel.2C_2004.E2.80.932013"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Research_vessel,_2004–2013">Research vessel, 2004–2013</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Research vessel, 2004–2013">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:OSV_Bold.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="683" data-file-width="1024" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/OSV_Bold.jpg/220px-OSV_Bold.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/OSV_Bold.jpg/330px-OSV_Bold.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/OSV_Bold.jpg/440px-OSV_Bold.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:OSV_Bold.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/USNS_Bold_(T-AGOS-12)" title="USNS Bold (T-AGOS-12)">OSV <i>Bold</i></a> docked at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Port_Canaveral,_Florida" title="Port Canaveral, Florida">Port Canaveral, Florida</a></div></div></div>
<p>In March 2004, the U.S. Navy transferred <a href="/wiki/USNS_Bold_(T-AGOS-12)" title="USNS Bold (T-AGOS-12)">USNS Bold (T-AGOS-12)</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stalwart_class_ocean_surveillance_ship" title="Stalwart class ocean surveillance ship"><i>Stalwart</i> class ocean surveillance ship</a>, to the EPA. The ship had been used in anti-submarine operations during the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>, was equipped with sidescan sonar, underwater video, water and sediment sampling instruments used in study of ocean and coastline. One of the major missions of the <i>Bold</i> was to monitor for ecological impact sites where materials were dumped from dredging operations in U.S. ports.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bold_brochure_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-bold_brochure-93">[93]</a></sup> In 2013, the <a href="/wiki/General_Services_Administration" title="General Services Administration">General Services Administration</a> sold the <i>Bold</i> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seattle_Central_Community_College" title="Seattle Central Community College">Seattle Central Community College</a> (SCCC), which demonstrated in a competition that they would put it to the highest and best purpose, at a nominal cost of $5,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Advance_identification">Advance identification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Advance identification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Advance identification, or ADID, is a planning process used by the EPA to identify wetlands and other bodies of water and their respective suitability for the discharge of dredged and fill material. The EPA conducts the process in cooperation with the U.S. <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Corps_of_Engineers" title="United States Army Corps of Engineers">Army Corps of Engineers</a> and local states or <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native American Tribes</a>. As of February 1993, 38 ADID projects had been completed and 33 were ongoing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Freedom_of_Information_Act_processing_performance">Freedom of Information Act processing performance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Freedom of Information Act processing performance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In the latest <a href="/wiki/Center_for_Effective_Government" title="Center for Effective Government">Center for Effective Government</a> analysis of 15 federal agencies which receive the most <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_Information_Act_(United_States)" title="Freedom of Information Act (United States)">Freedom of Information Act</a> FOIA requests, published in 2015 (using 2012 and 2013 data, the most recent years available), the EPA earned a D by scoring 67 out of a possible 100 points, i.e. did not earn a satisfactory overall grade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Controversies_.281983.E2.80.93present.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversies_(1983–present)">Controversies (1983–present)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Controversies (1983–present)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Epaheadquarters.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="941" data-file-width="1389" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Epaheadquarters.jpg/220px-Epaheadquarters.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Epaheadquarters.jpg/330px-Epaheadquarters.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Epaheadquarters.jpg/440px-Epaheadquarters.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Epaheadquarters.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>EPA headquarters in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span id="Fiscal_mismanagement.2C_1983"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Fiscal_mismanagement,_1983">Fiscal mismanagement, 1983</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Fiscal mismanagement, 1983">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1982 Congress charged that the EPA had mishandled the $1.6 billion program to clean up hazardous waste dumps <a href="/wiki/Superfund" title="Superfund">Superfund</a> and demanded records from EPA director <a href="/wiki/Anne_Gorsuch_Burford" title="Anne Gorsuch Burford">Anne M. Gorsuch</a>. She refused and became the first agency director in U.S. history to be cited for <a href="/wiki/Contempt_of_Congress" title="Contempt of Congress">contempt of Congress</a>. The EPA turned the documents over to Congress several months later, after the White House abandoned its court claim that the documents could not be subpoenaed by Congress because they were covered by <a href="/wiki/Executive_privilege" title="Executive privilege">executive privilege</a>. At that point, Gorsuch resigned her post, citing pressures caused by the media and the congressional investigation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toledoblade_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-toledoblade-97">[97]</a></sup> Critics charged that the EPA was in a shambles at that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spokanechronicle_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-spokanechronicle-98">[98]</a></sup> When Lee Thomas came to the agency in 1983 as Acting Assistant Administrator of the Office of Solid Waste and Emergency Response, shortly before Gorsuch's resignation, six congressional committees were investigating the Superfund program. There were also two FBI agents performing an investigation for the Justice Department into possible destruction of documents<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>.
</p><p>Gorsuch, appointed by <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a>, resigned under fire in 1983. Gorsuch based her administration of the EPA on the <a href="/wiki/New_Federalism" title="New Federalism">New Federalism</a> approach of downsizing federal agencies by delegating their functions and services to the individual states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epajournal_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-epajournal-100">[100]</a></sup> She believed that the EPA was over-regulating business and that the agency was too large and not cost-effective. During her 22 months as agency head, she cut the budget of the EPA by 22%, reduced the number of cases filed against polluters, relaxed Clean Air Act regulations, and facilitated the spraying of restricted-use pesticides. She cut the total number of agency employees, and hired staff from the industries they were supposed to be regulating.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wapo_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-wapo-101">[101]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Environmentalism" title="Environmentalism">Environmentalists</a> contended that her policies were designed to placate polluters, and accused her of trying to dismantle the agency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Political_pressure_and_scientific_integrity.2C_2001.E2.80.93present"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_pressure_and_scientific_integrity,_2001–present">Political pressure and scientific integrity, 2001–present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Political pressure and scientific integrity, 2001–present">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In April 2008, the <a href="/wiki/Union_of_Concerned_Scientists" title="Union of Concerned Scientists">Union of Concerned Scientists</a> said that more than half of the nearly 1,600 EPA staff scientists who responded online to a detailed questionnaire reported they had experienced incidents of political interference in their work. The survey included chemists, toxicologists, engineers, geologists and experts in other fields of science. About 40% of the scientists reported that the interference had been more prevalent in the last five years than in previous years. The highest number of complaints came from scientists who were involved in determining the risks of cancer by chemicals used in food and other aspects of everyday life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>EPA research has also been suppressed by career managers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Supervisors at EPA's National Center for Environmental Assessment required several paragraphs to be deleted from a peer-reviewed journal article about EPA's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Integrated_risk_information_system" title="Integrated risk information system">integrated risk information system</a>, which led two co-authors to have their names removed from the publication, and the corresponding author, Ching-Hung Hsu, to leave EPA "because of the draconian restrictions placed on publishing".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> EPA subjects employees who author scientific papers to <a href="/wiki/Prior_restraint" title="Prior restraint">prior restraint</a>, even if those papers are written on personal time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
