</p><p>EPA employees have reported difficulty in conducting and reporting the results of studies on <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_fracturing" title="Hydraulic fracturing">hydraulic fracturing</a> due to industry<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Urbina_03Mar2011_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Urbina_03Mar2011-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPA_re_study_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-EPA_re_study-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chesapeake_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chesapeake-109">[109]</a></sup> and governmental pressure, and are concerned about the censorship of environmental reports.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Urbina_03Mar2011_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-Urbina_03Mar2011-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT_lobbying_docs_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT_lobbying_docs-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT_Docs_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT_Docs-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2015, the <a href="/wiki/Government_Accountability_Office" title="Government Accountability Office">Government Accountability Office</a> stated that the EPA violated federal law with covert propaganda on their social media platforms. The social media messaging that was used promoted materials supporting the <a href="/wiki/Waters_of_the_United_States_Regulatory_Overreach_Protection_Act_of_2014" title="Waters of the United States Regulatory Overreach Protection Act of 2014">Waters of the United States</a> rule, including materials that were designed to oppose legislative efforts to limit or block the rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>In February 2017, U.S. Representative <a href="/wiki/Matt_Gaetz" title="Matt Gaetz">Matt Gaetz</a> (R-Fla.) sponsored <a href="/wiki/H.R._861_(115th_Congress)" title="H.R. 861 (115th Congress)">H.R. 861</a>, a bill<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> to abolish the EPA by 2018. According to Gaetz, "The American people are drowning in rules and regulation promulgated by unelected bureaucrats. And the Environmental Protection Agency has become an extraordinary offender." The bill was co-sponsored by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Massie" title="Thomas Massie">Thomas Massie</a> (R-Ky.), <a href="/wiki/Steven_Palazzo" title="Steven Palazzo">Steven Palazzo</a> (R-Ms.) and <a href="/wiki/Barry_Loudermilk" title="Barry Loudermilk">Barry Loudermilk</a> (R-Ga.).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Fuel_economy.2C_2005.E2.80.932010"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Fuel_economy,_2005–2010">Fuel economy, 2005–2010</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Fuel economy, 2005–2010">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In July 2005, an EPA report showing that auto companies were using loopholes to produce less fuel-efficient cars was delayed. The report was supposed to be released the day before a controversial energy bill was passed and would have provided backup for those opposed to it, but the EPA delayed its release at the last minute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2007, the state of California sued the EPA for its refusal to allow California and 16 other states to raise fuel economy standards for new cars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> EPA administrator <a href="/wiki/Stephen_L._Johnson" title="Stephen L. Johnson">Stephen L. Johnson</a> claimed that the EPA was working on its own standards, but the move has been widely considered an attempt to shield the auto industry from environmental regulation by setting lower standards at the federal level, which would then preempt state laws.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> California governor <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Schwarzenegger" title="Arnold Schwarzenegger">Arnold Schwarzenegger</a>, along with governors from 13 other states, stated that the EPA's actions ignored federal law, and that <i>existing</i> California standards (adopted by many states in addition to California) were almost twice as effective as the <i>proposed</i> federal standards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> It was reported that Stephen Johnson ignored his own staff in making this decision.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the federal government had bailed out <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chrysler" title="Chrysler">Chrysler</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Automotive_industry_crisis_of_2008%E2%80%932010" title="Automotive industry crisis of 2008–2010">Automotive industry crisis of 2008–2010</a>, the 2010 <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Equinox" title="Chevrolet Equinox">Chevrolet Equinox</a> was released with an EPA fuel economy rating abnormally higher than its competitors. Independent road tests<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> found that the vehicle did not out-perform its competitors, which had much lower fuel economy ratings. Later road tests found better, but inconclusive, results.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Mercury_emissions.2C_2005"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Mercury_emissions,_2005">Mercury emissions, 2005</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Mercury emissions, 2005">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In March 2005, nine states (California, New York, New Jersey, New Hampshire, Massachusetts, Maine, Connecticut, New Mexico and Vermont) sued the EPA. The EPA's <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Inspector_General_(United_States)" title="Office of Inspector General (United States)">Inspector General</a> had determined that the EPA's regulation of <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a> emissions did not follow the Clean Air Act, and that the regulations were influenced by top political appointees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> The EPA had suppressed a study it commissioned by <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a> which contradicted its position on mercury controls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> The suit alleged that the EPA's rule exempting coal-fired power plants from "maximum available control technology" was illegal, and additionally charged that the EPA's system of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cap-and-trade" title="Cap-and-trade">cap-and-trade</a> to lower average mercury levels would allow power plants to forego reducing mercury emissions, which they objected would lead to dangerous local hotspots of mercury contamination even if average levels declined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bustillo2005_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bustillo2005-131">[131]</a></sup> Several states also began to enact their own mercury emission regulations. Illinois's proposed rule would have reduced mercury emissions from power plants by an average of 90% by 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> In 2008—by which point a total of fourteen states had joined the suit—the U.S. Court of Appeals for the District of Columbia ruled that the EPA regulations violated the Clean Air Act.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baltimore2008_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baltimore2008-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p>In response, EPA announced plans to propose such standards to replace the vacated Clean Air Mercury Rule, and did so on March 16, 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPA_MATS2011_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-EPA_MATS2011-134">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Climate_change.2C_2007.E2.80.932017"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate_change,_2007–2017">Climate change, 2007–2017</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Climate change, 2007–2017">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Regulation_of_greenhouse_gases_under_the_Clean_Air_Act" title="Regulation of greenhouse gases under the Clean Air Act">Regulation of greenhouse gases under the Clean Air Act</a></div>
<table class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000">
<tbody><tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="415" data-file-width="759" height="22" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Wikinews-logo.svg/40px-Wikinews-logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Wikinews-logo.svg/60px-Wikinews-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Wikinews-logo.svg/80px-Wikinews-logo.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist">Wikinews has related news:
<i><b><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikinews.org/wiki/EPA_proposes_using_Clean_Air_Act_to_fight_global_warming" title="wikinews:EPA proposes using Clean Air Act to fight global warming"> EPA proposes using Clean Air Act to fight global warming</a></b></i></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In December 2007, EPA Administrator Stephen L. Johnson approved a draft of a document that declared that climate change imperiled the public welfare—a decision that would trigger the first national mandatory global-warming regulations. Associate Deputy Administrator Jason Burnett e-mailed the draft to the White House. White House aides—who had long resisted mandatory regulations as a way to address climate change—knew the gist of what Johnson's finding would be, Burnett said. They also knew that once they opened the attachment, it would become a public record, making it controversial and difficult to rescind. So they did not open it; rather, they called Johnson and asked him to take back the draft. Johnson rescinded the draft; in July 2008, he issued a new version which did not state that <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> was danger to public welfare. Burnett resigned in protest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p>A $3 million mapping study on <a href="/wiki/Climate_Change_Science_Program#Coastal_sensitivity_to_sea_level_rise_(SAP_4.1)" title="Climate Change Science Program"><i>sea level rise</i></a> was suppressed by EPA management during both the Bush and Obama Administrations, and managers changed a key interagency report to reflect the removal of the maps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup>
</p><p>On April 28, 2017, multiple climate change subdomains at EPA.gov began redirecting to a notice stating "this page is being updated."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> The EPA issued a statement announcing the overhaul of its website to "reflect the agency's new direction under President Donald Trump and Administrator Scott Pruitt."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> The removed EPA climate change domains included extensive information on the EPA's work to mitigate climate change, as well as details of data collection efforts and indicators for climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Gold_King_Mine_waste_water_spill.2C_2015"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Gold_King_Mine_waste_water_spill,_2015">Gold King Mine waste water spill, 2015</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Gold King Mine waste water spill, 2015">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In August 2015, the <a href="/wiki/2015_Gold_King_Mine_waste_water_spill" title="2015 Gold King Mine waste water spill">2015 Gold King Mine waste water spill</a> occurred when EPA contractors examined the level of pollutants such as <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Colorado" title="Colorado">Colorado</a> mine,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> and accidentally released over three million gallons of waste water into Cement Creek and the <a href="/wiki/Animas_River" title="Animas River">Animas River</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aug10spillfox_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-aug10spillfox-141">[141]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Collusion_with_Monsanto_chemical_company">Collusion with Monsanto chemical company</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Collusion with Monsanto chemical company">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2015, the <a href="/wiki/International_Agency_for_Research_on_Cancer" title="International Agency for Research on Cancer">International Agency for Research on Cancer</a> (IARC), a branch of the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a>, cited research linking <a href="/wiki/Glyphosate" title="Glyphosate">glyphosate</a>, an ingredient of the weed killer Roundup manufactured by the chemical company <a href="/wiki/Monsanto" title="Monsanto">Monsanto</a>, to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-Hodgkin%27s_lymphoma" title="Non-Hodgkin's lymphoma">non-Hodgkin's lymphoma</a>.  In March 2017, the presiding judge in a litigation brought about by people who claim to have developed glyphosate-related non-Hodgkin's lymphoma opened Monsanto emails and other documents related to the case, including email exchanges between the company and federal regulators.  According to an article in the <i>New York Times</i>, the "records suggested that Monsanto had ghostwritten research that was later attributed to academics and indicated that a senior official at the Environmental Protection Agency had worked to quash a review of Roundup’s main ingredient, glyphosate, that was to have been conducted by the United States Department of Health and Human Services."  The records show that Monsanto was able to prepare "a public relations assault" on the finding after they were alerted to the determination by Jess Rowland, the head of the EPA’s cancer assessment review committee  at that time,  months in advance.  Emails also showed that Rowland "had promised to beat back an effort by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Department_of_Health_and_Human_Services" title="Department of Health and Human Services">Department of Health and Human Services</a> to conduct its own review."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Chief_Scott_Pruitt.2C_2017"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chief_Scott_Pruitt,_2017">Chief Scott Pruitt, 2017</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Chief Scott Pruitt, 2017">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On February 17, 2017, <a href="/wiki/Scott_Pruitt" title="Scott Pruitt">Scott Pruitt</a> was selected <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Administrator_of_the_EPA" title="Administrator of the EPA">Administrator of the EPA</a> by president <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a>. This was a seemingly controversial move, as Pruitt had spent most of his career countering environmental policy. He did not have previous experience in the field and had received financial support from the fossil fuel industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pruitt resigned from the position on July 5, 2018, citing "unrelenting attacks" due to ongoing ethics controversies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p>
